### Question 1: CLAIMS-1

**Query:** How do I file a VA disability claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 7.91s

**Answer:**

To file a VA disability claim, you have several options:

- File online yourself at VA.gov by completing the claim form (VA Form 21-526EZ) available [here](https://www.va.gov/disability/how-to-file-claim/) or directly submit the form [here](https://eauth.va.gov/accessva/?cspSelectFor=quicksubmit).
- File with the assistance of a Veterans Service Officer (VSO), who can help you prepare and submit your claim. You can find a VSO near you [here](https://www.va.gov/ogc/apps/accreditation/index.asp).
- File by fax at 844-531-7818.
- File in person by submitting the completed VA Form 21-526EZ to your nearest VA Regional Office.
- File by mail by sending the completed VA Form 21-526EZ to:

  ```
  Department of Veterans Affairs
  Claims Intake Center
  PO Box 4444
  Janesville, WI 53547-4444
  ```

Important notes:

- If you file a new claim while another claim is active, the VA will close the newly filed claim automatically but will eventually combine your new issues with the older active claim.
- The VA uses the date the claim is processed by the intake center, not the postmark date, to establish the effective date of your claim.
- If you are filing a Fully Developed Claim (BDD), you must sign the claim paperwork yourself if it is your first claim.
- You do not need to have a diagnosis to file a claim.

It is strongly recommended to use a VSO to assist with filing your claim to ensure it is done correctly³⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing a VA Disability Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
² [Filing a BDD Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
³ [Filing a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
⁴ [How to File](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Filing a VA Disability Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [Filing a BDD Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [Filing a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

### Question 2: CLAIMS-2

**Query:** What forms do I need to file a claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 5.49s

**Answer:**

To file a VA disability claim, the primary form you need is:

- VA Form 21-526EZ, "Application for Disability Compensation and Related Compensation Benefits"¹

For other types of claims, such as dependency and indemnity compensation or accrued benefits, you may need:

- VA Form 21P-534EZ, "Application for Dependency and Indemnity Compensation, Death Pension and Accrued Benefits"  
- VA Form 21P-535, "Application for Dependency and Indemnity Compensation by Parent(s)"  
- VA Form 21P-601, "Application for Accrued Amounts Due a Deceased Beneficiary"  
- VA Form 21P-0847, "Request for Substitution of Claimant Upon Death Claimant" (can be submitted online)³

If you are filing a claim for accelerated benefits related to insurance, you would use:

- Claim for Accelerated Benefits (SGLV 8284)⁷

You can file these forms online, by fax, mail, or in person at a VA Regional Office¹.

**Sources:**

¹ [Filing a Claim - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
³ [How to File - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

### Question 4: CLAIMS-4

**Query:** What is a C&P exam?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.19s

**Answer:**

A C&P (Compensation and Pension) exam is a medical examination scheduled by the VA as part of the disability claims process. It is used to evaluate the severity and service connection of a Veteran's claimed disabilities. Veterans can request specific contractors, VA medical centers, or tele-health appointments for their C&P exams, and the VA will attempt to honor these requests if reasonable and possible. After submitting a claim, Veterans can expect to be scheduled for a C&P exam, although in some cases, it may take several months or may not be scheduled at all. It is important for Veterans to keep their contact information up-to-date to avoid missing notices about their exam¹⁵.

Additionally, to understand how a C&P exam will be conducted, Veterans are encouraged to review the applicable Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQ), which outlines the criteria and examination process for specific conditions. Veterans may also have a physician complete a DBQ on their behalf to submit with their claim²³⁴⁶⁷.

**Sources:**
¹ [Compensation & Pension Examinations (C&P)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
² [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
³ [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
⁴ [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
⁵ [After Submitting a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
⁶ [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
⁷ [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)

### Question 5: CLAIMS-5

**Query:** How do I prepare for a C&P exam?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 6.45s

**Answer:**

To prepare for a Compensation and Pension (C&P) exam, consider the following steps:

- Review the applicable Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQ) related to your condition. This will give you an idea of what the exam will cover and how it will be conducted. You can find DBQs for various conditions such as cervical spine, thoracolumbar spine, traumatic brain injury (TBI), cranial nerve conditions, arthritis, fibromyalgia, osteomyelitis, breast conditions, and gynecological conditions³⁴⁵⁶⁷.

- If you want, you may ask your treating physician to complete a DBQ on your behalf to submit with your claim. This can help provide detailed medical information to the VA⁶.

- You can include a personal statement with your request if you want to use a specific contractor or VA medical center for your exam, or if you prefer a tele-health appointment for your mental health exam. The VA will attempt to honor these requests if reasonable and possible¹.

- Keep your mailing address, email address, and phone number up-to-date with the VA to ensure you receive notice of your exam appointment⁴.

- Be prepared that the exam may be scheduled shortly after you file your claim, but sometimes it may take several months or you may not get one at all⁴.

These steps will help you be better prepared for your C&P exam.

**Sources:**

¹ [Compensation & Pension Examinations (C&P)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
² [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs) - Cervical Spine](https://www.benefits.va.gov/compensation/docs/Neck_Conditions_Cervical_Spine.pdf)  
³ [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs) - TBI (OLD)](https://www.dcms.uscg.mil/Portals/10/CG-1/PSC/PSD/docs/VBA%20-%20Initial%20evaluation%20of%20TBI%20residuals.pdf?ver=2017-03-28-105323-973)  
⁴ [After Submitting a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
⁵ [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs) - Cranial Nerve Conditions](https://www.benefits.va.gov/compensation/docs/Cranial_Nerve_Conditions.pdf)  
⁶ [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs) - Arthritis, Fibromyalgia, Osteomyelitis](https://www.benefits.va.gov/compensation/docs/Arthritis.pdf)  
⁷ [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs) - Breast and Gynecological Conditions](https://www.benefits.va.gov/compensation/docs/Breast_Conditions.pdf)

### Question 6: CLAIMS-6

**Query:** What happens after I file a claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 2.43s

**Answer:**

After you file a claim, the next step typically involves being scheduled for a Compensation & Pension Exam (C&P exam). This exam is part of the claims process and is used to evaluate the severity of your claimed conditions. Veterans can expect to be scheduled for this exam shortly after filing their claim, although in some cases, the exam may not be scheduled at all or could be delayed for several months. It is important to keep your mailing address, email address, and phone number up-to-date with the VA to ensure you receive all notices about the examination¹.

### Question 7: CLAIMS-7

**Query:** Can I track my claim status?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 2.17s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can track your claim status. For appeals, you can check the status on VA.gov, by calling 1 (800) 827-1000, or by making a VERA appointment. For supplemental claims, claim trackers will show statuses such as "We don't know your status" or "A reviewer is examining your new evidence" until the claim is closed out.

### Question 8: CLAIMS-8

**Query:** What is an Intent to File?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 3.91s

**Answer:**

An Intent to File is the first step in filing a VA disability claim. It is important because it preserves your effective date, which determines when you start receiving payments from the VA. For recently separated veterans, an Intent to File can be automatically opened the day after separation, and if filed before the one-year separation anniversary, it allows up to an additional year to submit the claim while still receiving back pay from the day after separation¹.

An Intent to File can be submitted in several ways, including online at VA.gov, through a Veterans Service Officer (VSO), by phone, in person using VA Form 21-0966, by fax, or by mail. The VA uses the date the Intent to File is processed, not the postmark date, to establish the effective date. An Intent to File is valid for one year and can be used for one claim, although that claim can cover multiple conditions. If multiple claims or appeals are filed on the same day, they all share the same Intent to File².

**Sources:**
¹ [Intent to File - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
² [Starting an Intent to File - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Intent to File](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [Starting an Intent to File](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

**Confidence:** 0.6868815422058105

### Question 9: CLAIMS-9

**Query:** How do I get my service records?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 7.81s

**Answer:**

To get your military service records, including awards, DD-214, enlistment paperwork, orders, and personnel actions, the method depends on your branch of service and when you separated:

- For Air Force, Army, Marine Corps, Navy, Coast Guard, NOAA, Public Health Services, and Space Force, the process varies by separation date.
- If your branch and separation date fall under the category marked with an asterisk (*), you need to submit a request to the National Personnel Records Center (NPRC) at https://vetrecs.archives.gov/s/veteran-request.
- If your branch and separation date fall under the category marked with an at symbol (@), you can request your personnel file via the Defense Personnel Records Information System (DPRIS) by logging in at https://milconnect.dmdc.osd.mil/milconnect/protected/portlet/dpris and navigating to Correspondence/Documentation > Defense Personnel Records Information (DPRIS) > Request Personnel File.

Here is a summary table from the source for quick reference:

| Branch of Service | Period of Service Ended | How to Obtain |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Air Force | Before 10/1/2004 | NPRC request (*) |
| Air Force | After 9/30/2004 | DPRIS (@) |
| Army | Before 10/1/1994 | NPRC request (*) |
| Army | After 9/30/1994 | DPRIS (@) |
| Coast Guard | Any | NPRC request (*) |
| Marine Corps | Before 1/1/1999 | NPRC request (*) |
| Marine Corps | After 12/31/1998 | DPRIS (@) |
| Navy | Before 1/1/1995 | NPRC request (*) |
| Navy | After 12/31/1994 | DPRIS (@) |
| Space Force | Any | DPRIS (@) |

If you want your Service Treatment Records (medical records), the process depends on your branch and period of service and may involve submitting an SF-180 form or accessing records through the MHS GENESIS Patient Portal for recent service (on or after 4/1/2025).

**Summary:**

- Submit a request to NPRC if your service period and branch fall under the NPRC category.
- Use DPRIS online system if your service period and branch fall under the DPRIS category.
- For medical records, follow the specific instructions by branch and service period, including possible use of SF-180 or MHS GENESIS portal.

**Sources:**

¹ [Military Personnel Records](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/infect)  
² [Service Treatment Records (STRs)/Military Medical Records](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/infect)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Military Personnel Records](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/infect)
- [Service Treatment Records (STRs)/Military Medical Records](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/infect)
- [Viewing Exam Reports](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [Obtaining Records](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#obtainingrecords)
- [Buddy Letters and Statements - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/buddy#faq)

### Question 10: CLAIMS-10

**Query:** What is a buddy statement?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 2.63s

**Answer:**

A buddy statement, also known as a buddy letter, is a written statement provided by someone who can attest to a veteran's military service, changes in behavior, or the severity of symptoms related to a claim. It is used as evidence to support a VA disability claim when military records are missing evidence or context. The statement can be written by someone who served with the veteran, friends and family, civilian coworkers or supervisors, or others such as a parole officer or priest.

**Sources:**
¹ [Buddy Letters and Statements - (claims_evidence)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/buddy#buddylettersandstatements)

### Question 11: CLAIMS-11

**Query:** Do I need a lawyer to file a claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 2.38s

**Answer:**

You do not need a lawyer to file a VA claim. Hiring an attorney or claims agent is a personal decision. Unless your appeal depends on a specific legal issue or angle, the benefit of having a lawyer may not be significant¹. However, for certain claims like federal tort claims, it is highly recommended to seek an attorney who specializes in that area, as these claims require you to do all the leg work and gather evidence yourself².

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)  
² [Winning a Federal Tort Claim - (claims_special)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#winningafederaltortclaim)

### Question 12: CLAIMS-12

**Query:** What is a nexus letter?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 2.73s

**Answer:**

A nexus letter is a written statement from a medically licensed and board-certified doctor that supports a veteran's disability claim by linking (or "nexusing") the veteran's current medical condition to their military service. The letter typically includes the doctor's credentials, the veteran's diagnosis, a review of the veteran's medical and service records, and an explanation of how the veteran's military service caused or contributed to their condition. It may also address other potential risk factors and explain why they are less likely to be the cause of the condition than the veteran's service. The purpose of the nexus letter is to provide a clear medical opinion that establishes the connection between the veteran's disability and their service, which is crucial for VA claims approval.

**Sources:**
¹ [Independent Medical Opinion (IMO) / Nexus Letter - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/imo#faq)  
² [Nexus Letter Example](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/imo#nexusletterexample)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Independent Medical Opinion (IMO) / Nexus Letter - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/imo#faq)
- [Nexus Letter Example](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/imo#nexusletterexample)

### Question 13: CLAIMS-13

**Query:** How do I get a nexus letter?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 3.14s

**Answer:**

To get a nexus letter, you should ask a medically licensed and board-certified doctor who is familiar with your medical history and military service to write a statement supporting your disability claim. The letter should include:

- The doctor's credentials and specialty.
- Confirmation that you are their patient and the date since when.
- Your diagnosis with supporting medical evidence (e.g., lab work, X-rays).
- A review of your medical history, including service treatment records and other relevant medical records.
- A connection between your current condition and specific events or circumstances during your military service.
- An explanation about the absence of other risk factors or why other factors are less likely to have caused your condition.

While the letter does not need to be on official letterhead, having it on letterhead with the doctor's practice information can add authority and authenticity to the letter.

**Sources:**
¹ [Nexus Letter Example](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/imo#nexusletterexample)  
² [Independent Medical Opinion (IMO) / Nexus Letter - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/imo#faq)

**Sources Cited:**

- [Independent Medical Opinion (IMO) / Nexus Letter - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/imo#faq)

### Question 14: CLAIMS-14

**Query:** What evidence do I need for my claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 6.18s

**Answer:**

For your VA disability claim, you need evidence that supports three essential elements for a successful claim:

- A current diagnosis of a chronic disability.
- An in-service event, exposure, or service-connected disability that caused or worsened your disability.
- A medical opinion (nexus statement) connecting the current diagnosis to the in-service event or exposure¹.

You can submit various types of evidence, including:

- Medical records showing your diagnosis and treatment.
- Evidence of the in-service event or exposure, such as service records or personal statements.
- Medical opinions or nexus letters from doctors linking your condition to your military service.
- New and relevant evidence if you are filing a supplemental claim, such as new medical exams, independent medical opinions, DBQs, personal or buddy statements².
- Buddy statements from family, friends, or service members who can attest to your condition and its impact³.
- Supporting or personal statements to add context or highlight important details in your records³.

If you have private medical records or non-VA hospital reports, you must provide VA Form 21-4142/21-4142a to authorize VA to obtain them³.

When filing for PTSD specifically, VA Form 21-0781 (Supporting Statements for Service-connection for PTSD) is generally required³.

Remember, you do not need to have all evidence at the time of filing, as VA can assist in developing your claim, but having at least the diagnosis and evidence of the in-service event before filing increases your chances of success¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Successful Claims](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
² [Supplemental Claim (20-0995)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
³ [Miscellaneous Evidence](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 15: CLAIMS-15

**Query:** Can I file a claim while still on active duty?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 3.79s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can file a VA disability claim while still on active duty. If you have 90-180 days before separation, you may file a Benefits Delivery at Discharge (BDD) claim, which ideally allows you to get a claims decision the day after you separate. Even if you have less than 90 days before separation, you can and should still file a claim. To file a claim yourself, you can go to VA.gov. It is also recommended that before you separate from service, you obtain a copy of your military medical records and seek treatment for your disabilities to have a diagnosis before separation.

### Question 16: CLAIMS-16

**Query:** What is BDD (Benefits Delivery at Discharge)?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.37s

**Answer:**

Benefits Delivery at Discharge (BDD) is a VA program that allows active duty servicemembers who are between 180 and 90 days before separation to file a disability claim within that window. The goal of BDD is to enable the claim to be processed and a decision to be made by the day after the veteran separates from active duty. This program is designed to give the claim a head start before separation, although it is not guaranteed that a decision will be made immediately upon separation.

Key points about BDD include:

- You must submit your Service Treatment Records (STRs) no later than 90 days before separation to remain in the BDD program.
- Private medical records are not automatically included in STRs and must be added by the veteran.
- You do not need to complete all exams before separation or before the 90-day window closes.
- Keeping the VA updated on your location is important for receiving notifications about examinations.
- If you have less than 90 days before separation, you can still file a claim, but it may not be processed under BDD.
- Veterans can file BDD claims online themselves or with assistance from a Veterans Service Officer (VSO).
- You do not need a diagnosis to file a claim under BDD.

BDD is intended to streamline the disability claims process for servicemembers transitioning out of active duty, ideally providing a faster decision on their claims right after separation¹⁴².

**Sources:**
¹ [Benefits Delivery at Discharge (BDD) - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/digsystem)  
² [Filing a BDD Claim - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
⁴ [Active Duty Servicemembers - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)

### Question 17: CLAIMS-17

**Query:** How do I file for an increase?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 2.37s

**Answer:**

To file for an increase in your VA disability rating:

- Review the VA Rating Schedule to determine if your current disability level qualifies for an increase.
- File your increase claim on the va.gov website.
- The VA will order new exams to assess the current severity of your condition(s).
- You do not need to supply evidence that your condition has worsened.
- Be aware that filing for an increase opens your entire claims file for review, which can result in corrections to any errors in your file, but most raters focus only on the condition(s) for which you request the increase¹.

### Question 18: CLAIMS-18

**Query:** What is a supplemental claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.13s

**Answer:**

A supplemental claim is an opportunity for a veteran to submit **new and relevant** evidence to the VA to support a previously denied claim. "New" means the VA did not previously have or consider the evidence, and "relevant" means the evidence directly addresses the reason for the denial. For example, if a claim was denied due to lack of diagnosis, submitting evidence of a diagnosis would be relevant.

New and relevant evidence can include:

- Being seen by a doctor for the claimed issue(s)
- A new theory of service connection (such as claiming the condition was caused by another service-connected disability)
- Evidence related to presumptive conditions
- Willingness to attend new VA examinations if previously missed
- Independent medical opinions or nexus letters
- Disability Benefits Questionnaires (DBQs)
- Personal or buddy statements

Veterans have **1 year** from the decision on their claim to file a supplemental claim to potentially keep their back pay. It is recommended to wait until enough evidence is gathered before filing. Supplemental claims are reviewed by a VA rater, and it is typical to receive new exams or medical opinions when filing a supplemental claim, provided the new evidence properly addresses the denial rationale.

**Sources:**

¹ [Supplemental Claim (20-0995)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
² [Supplemental Claim - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#supplementalclaim)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Supplemental Claim [(20-0995)](http://www.vba.va.gov/pubs/forms/VBA-20-0995-ARE.pdf)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)
- [Supplemental Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#supplementalclaim)

### Question 20: CLAIMS-20

**Query:** Can I reopen a denied claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 5.45s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can reopen a denied claim by filing an appeal. One way to do this is by submitting a Supplemental Claim, which allows you to provide new and relevant evidence that was not previously considered. New evidence can include new medical records, a new diagnosis, an independent medical opinion (IMO)/nexus letter, personal or buddy statements, or willingness to attend a new exam if you missed a previous one. You have 1 year from the decision on your claim to file an appeal and submit this additional evidence. If you believe the VA did not properly evaluate your evidence, you might also consider filing a Higher-Level Review (HLR). If an error is found during HLR that requires new evidence, your HLR may be administratively closed and reopened as a supplemental claim, allowing you to submit new evidence.  

It is important not to file a new claim for a condition that was previously denied or a similar condition, as this will likely be rejected and you will be directed to appeal instead.  

In summary, reopening a denied claim is done through an appeal process such as a Supplemental Claim or HLR, with the opportunity to submit new and relevant evidence to support your case³⁴⁵⁶⁷.

**Sources:**
¹ [Supplemental Claim (20-0995)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
² [Denied Service-Connection - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#deniedserviceconnection)  
³ [Denied Service-Connection - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

### Question 22: CLAIMS-22

**Query:** What is aggravation?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 5.19s

**Answer:**

Aggravation in the context of VA disability claims refers to the worsening of a condition due to military service or a service-connected condition. There are two types of aggravation:

1. Aggravation of a preexisting (pre-service) condition by military service.
2. Aggravation of an existing (post-service) condition by a service-connected condition.

This means if a condition you had before or after service gets worse because of your military service or a service-connected disability, that worsening can be considered for service connection under aggravation¹.

To establish aggravation, the VA must determine the level of severity of the condition before and after the aggravation. Medical evidence showing the severity before aggravation is required to prove how much the condition worsened due to service or a service-connected condition².

Rating of aggravated conditions involves subtracting the severity of the preexisting condition from the current severity to determine the aggravated rating. For preexisting conditions, if the condition was rated 100% before aggravation, the final rating remains 100%. This does not apply to aggravations of conditions after separation from service³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Service-Connection by Aggravation](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#serviceconnectionbyaggravation)  
² [Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofanexistingpostservicecondition)  
³ [Rating Aggravated Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#ratingaggravatedconditions)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Service-Connection by Aggravation](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#serviceconnectionbyaggravation)
- [Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofanexistingpostservicecondition)
- [Service-Connection by Aggravation - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#references)
- [Aggravation of a Preexisting (Pre-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofapreexistingpreservicecondition)
- [Rating Aggravated Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#ratingaggravatedconditions)

### Question 23: CLAIMS-23

**Query:** How do I prove service connection?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.26s

**Answer:**

To prove service connection for a VA disability claim, you need to establish the following:

- A current diagnosis of the disability.
- An in-service event, injury, or disease that caused or contributed to the disability.
- A medical nexus showing that it is at least as likely as not (50% or greater probability) that the disability is related to your military service or a service-connected condition.

If any of these elements are missing, your claim may be denied. To strengthen your claim, you can submit additional evidence such as:

- An Independent Medical Opinion (IMO) or Nexus letter linking your disability to service.
- Personal statements explaining your condition and its connection to service.
- Buddy statements from fellow service members who can attest to your in-service injury or symptoms.

If you were denied because you missed a VA exam, you can file a supplemental claim and state that you are willing and able to attend exams at this time.

If you believe the VA did not properly evaluate your evidence, you may consider filing a Higher-Level Review (HLR) to have a different rater review your claim.

These steps help establish and prove service connection for your disability claim.

**Sources:**
¹ [Denied Service-Connection - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#deniedserviceconnection)  
² [Denied Service-Connection - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Denied Service-Connection](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#deniedserviceconnection)
- [Denied Service-Connection](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)
- [Service-Connection by Aggravation](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#serviceconnectionbyaggravation)

### Question 26: APPEAL-1

**Query:** How do I appeal a VA decision?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 5.95s

**Answer:**

To appeal a VA decision, you can follow these steps:

- If you disagree with your claim's decision, you may file an appeal.
- You can use the assistance of a Veterans Service Organization (VSO), lawyer, or an accredited claims agent.
- There are three initial methods to appeal:
  1. Higher-Level Review
  2. Supplemental Claim
  3. Board of Veterans Appeals (BVA)
- You must file your appeal within one year of your claim's decision date to keep your effective date (back pay).
- You cannot file multiple appeals for the same issue at the same time without withdrawing the current appeal.
- If you want to submit an appeal to the BVA, you can do so online, via your VSO or lawyer, by fax, or by mail.
- If your appeal is denied again, you can try another eligible option.
- If you lost your BVA appeal but have new and relevant evidence, you can file a Supplemental Claim or appeal to the U.S. Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims.

For checking the status of your appeal, you can visit VA.gov, call 1-800-827-1000, or make a VERA appointment³⁴⁵⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)  
² [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)  
³ [Filing an Appeal](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#filinganappeal)  
⁴ [Filing an Appeal](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
⁵ [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)  
⁶ [Submitting to BVA](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)
- [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)
- [Filing an Appeal](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#filinganappeal)

### Question 27: APPEAL-2

**Query:** What is a Higher Level Review?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 4.25s

**Answer:**

A Higher-Level Review (HLR) is an entirely new review of your VA disability claim conducted by an experienced, more senior rater known as a Decision Review Officer (DRO). This review involves re-examining the evidence already in the record but does **not** allow for the submission or consideration of any new evidence received after the original decision was made. The DRO has the authority to issue a "difference of opinion," meaning they can weigh the existing evidence differently and potentially reach a more favorable outcome for the veteran. However, such differences of opinion cannot result in a negative outcome for the veteran. If the previous decision contained Clear and Unmistakable Errors (CUEs), those errors can be corrected even if the correction results in a negative outcome, with appropriate due notice.

Additional important points about Higher-Level Review include:

- Veterans cannot file back-to-back HLRs but can alternate between HLRs and supplemental claims.
- Veterans cannot request an HLR after a Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA) denial.
- The HLR must be submitted within one year of the decision being reviewed.
- Intent to file does not apply to HLRs.

In summary, an HLR is a senior rater's fresh look at your claim based on the existing evidence, aiming to correct any errors or differences in opinion without considering new evidence¹³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Higher-Level Review (HLR 20-0996)](https://www.vba.va.gov/pubs/forms/VBA-20-0996-ARE.pdf)  
³ [Higher-Level Review - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#higherlevelreview)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Higher-Level Review [(HLR 20-0996)](https://www.vba.va.gov/pubs/forms/VBA-20-0996-ARE.pdf)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [1\. Direct Review](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [Higher-Level Review](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#higherlevelreview)

### Question 28: APPEAL-3

**Query:** What is a Supplemental Claim?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 5.49s

**Answer:**

A Supplemental Claim is an opportunity for a veteran to submit **new and relevant** evidence to the VA to support their claim for disability benefits. "New" means the VA did not previously have or consider the evidence, and "relevant" means the evidence directly addresses the reason for the denial of the claim. For example, if a claim was denied due to lack of diagnosis, submitting evidence of a diagnosis would be relevant.

New and relevant evidence can include:

- Being seen by a doctor for the claimed issue(s)
- A new theory of service connection (such as claiming the condition was caused by another service-connected disability)
- Evidence related to presumptive conditions
- Willingness to attend new VA examinations if previously missed
- Independent medical opinions or nexus letters
- Disability Benefits Questionnaires (DBQs)
- Personal statements or buddy statements

Veterans have 1 year from the date of the claim's decision to file a Supplemental Claim to potentially preserve their effective date for back pay. It is recommended to wait until enough evidence is gathered before filing. The Supplemental Claim can be submitted online, by paper, through a Veterans Service Organization (VSO) or lawyer, in person, or by mail. The VA will not consider the postmark date but the date the claim is processed by the VA's intake center, so timely submission is critical.

Typically, filing a Supplemental Claim may result in new VA exams or medical opinions, but these will only be ordered if the new evidence properly addresses the reason for the denial.

In summary, a Supplemental Claim is a formal way to provide additional evidence to support a denied claim within a year of the decision, aiming to get the claim granted or increased based on this new information³⁶.

**Sources:**

¹ [Supplemental Claim (20-0995)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
² [Supplemental Claim - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#supplementalclaim)  
³ [Submitting a Supplemental Claim - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
⁶ [Filing an Appeal - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Supplemental Claim [(20-0995)](http://www.vba.va.gov/pubs/forms/VBA-20-0995-ARE.pdf)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)

### Question 29: APPEAL-4

**Query:** What is the Board of Veterans Appeals?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 4.61s

**Answer:**

The Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA) is a review body where Veterans Law Judges review appeals from veterans regarding their VA claims decisions. These judges are not bound by VA Central Office policy decisions and have the authority to issue grants that VA raters cannot, which means their decisions can sometimes contradict VA policies. The BVA appeal process typically takes years to complete and is usually chosen after other appeal options like Supplemental Claim or Higher Level Review have been exhausted. Veterans must submit their appeal to the BVA within one year of their claim's decision and cannot request a BVA appeal after receiving a BVA decision for the same claimed issue consecutively.

There are three lanes or appeal dockets a veteran can choose when appealing to the BVA:

1. Direct Review – a judge reviews the claim without new evidence.
2. Evidence Submission – allows submission of new evidence for the judge to review.
3. Hearing – the veteran can have a hearing before a Veterans Law Judge.

The BVA judges review the case and issue decisions that may grant benefits or deny the claim based on their interpretation of the law and evidence presented.

**Sources:**
¹ [Board Veterans' Appeals (BVA) AKA, Notice of Disagreement (NOD), AKA](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
² [Board Veterans' Appeals (BVA) AKA, Notice of Disagreement (NOD), AKA - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#boardveteransappealsbvaakanoticeofdisagreementnodaka)

**Sources Cited:**

- [Board Veterans' Appeals (BVA) AKA, Notice of Disagreement (NOD), AKA](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#boardveteransappealsbvaakanoticeofdisagreementnodaka)

### Question 32: APPEAL-7

**Query:** Can I get a hearing for my appeal?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 1.68s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get a hearing for your appeal. The hearing can be conducted by videoconference or in-person in Washington, DC. After the hearing, you will have 90 days to submit any new evidence. The judge will then make a decision considering both the hearing and the evidence of record¹.

### Question 33: APPEAL-8

**Query:** What is a Board hearing?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 1.96s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **48.9%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

A Board hearing is an opportunity for a veteran to present their case before a Veterans Law Judge either by videoconference or in-person in Washington, DC. After the hearing, the veteran has 90 days to submit any new evidence. The judge will then make a decision based on the hearing and the evidence of record¹. This hearing is one of the three appeal lanes available when appealing to the Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA)².

### Question 34: APPEAL-9

**Query:** Should I get a lawyer for my appeal?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 2.26s

**Answer:**

Whether you should get a lawyer for your appeal is a personal decision. According to the information provided, unless your appeal hinges on a specific legal issue or angle, the benefit a lawyer or claims agent provides may not be significant. You may also use the assistance of a Veterans Service Organization (VSO), lawyer, or an accredited claims agent when filing an appeal. Keep in mind that lawyers and claims agents cannot charge more than 33.3% of your back pay and only get paid if you win your appeal. VSOs provide free assistance to veterans, which can be a valuable resource¹³.

### Question 36: APPEAL-11

**Query:** What is the difference between legacy and AMA appeals?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 3.43s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **49.6%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

The provided context does not explicitly define or explain the difference between legacy and AMA (Appeals Modernization Act) appeals. However, it does mention that the "legacy appeal" process is the older appeals system, and there is an option to convert or "opt-in" from legacy appeals to the modern appeal processes (AMA). This opt-in box only applies to veterans who have been in a continuous appeal since around 2017; otherwise, it can be ignored¹.

In summary:
- Legacy appeals refer to the older, traditional appeals system.
- AMA appeals refer to the modernized appeals process introduced after 2017.
- Veterans in legacy appeals can choose to convert their appeal to the AMA process by opting in on the supplemental claim form, but this only applies if they have been in continuous appeal since about 2017¹.

No further details about the procedural or substantive differences between legacy and AMA appeals are provided in the context.

### Question 38: APPEAL-13

**Query:** Can I submit new evidence on appeal?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 2.76s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can submit new evidence on appeal. When your appeal becomes a supplemental claim, you are allowed to submit any new and relevant evidence to support your claim¹. Additionally, if you file a Notice of Disagreement (NOD), you have 90 days from the day your NOD is received to submit new evidence². New and relevant evidence means evidence that the VA did not previously have or consider and that directly addresses the reasons for the denial, such as new medical opinions, personal statements, or additional medical examinations⁵.

### Question 40: APPEAL-15

**Query:** How do I choose an appeal lane?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 4.09s

**Answer:**

The context provided does not contain specific information on how to choose an appeal lane. However, it does mention that when filing a Board Appeal, you can select different Board review options by submitting one Board Appeal form with additional pages explaining your preferences or by submitting multiple forms for each review option. Also, you can change your review option at the Board by filling out a new Board Appeal form, checking the box for the new review option, and listing all issues, but this must be done within one year from the date on the decision or 60 days from submitting the Board Appeal, whichever is later¹ ⁶.

For more detailed guidance on choosing an appeal lane, you might want to carefully read your Decision Letter, which should outline what you need to do for a successful appeal, including any missing evidence or exams² ⁴ ⁷.

### Question 41: APPEAL-16

**Query:** What is the Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 2.67s

**Answer:**

The Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims (CAVC) is a court where veterans can appeal decisions made by the Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA). The judges at the CAVC have broad authority to interpret the laws and regulations that govern the Veterans Benefits Administration (VBA), and their decisions create case law that can change how the VBA operates. Veterans have 120 days after their BVA decision to file an appeal directly with the CAVC, not with the VA. While veterans are allowed to represent themselves, it is strongly recommended they seek legal representation at this stage¹³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims (CAVC) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
³ [Higher Courts (Courts above BVA) - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#highercourtscourtsabovebva)

- [Board Veterans' Appeals (BVA) AKA, Notice of Disagreement (NOD), AKA [10182](https://www.va.gov/vaforms/va/pdf/VA10182.pdf)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

### Question 42: APPEAL-17

**Query:** Can I appeal a C&P exam?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 4.26s

**Answer:**

The context does not explicitly state whether you can appeal a C&P (Compensation and Pension) exam itself. However, it does provide information about appealing claim decisions related to C&P exams:

- If you disagree with your claim's decision (which may be based on the C&P exam results), you may file an appeal using one of three methods: Higher-Level Review, Supplemental Claim, or Board of Veterans Appeals (BVA)¹.
- During an appeal, you may be sent for another C&P exam if the VA finds a duty to assist error or if you file a supplemental claim²³.
- Filing an appeal invites the VA to review your file and potentially reduce or adjust your ratings if errors or improvements are found⁶.

But there is no direct mention of appealing the exam itself as a separate action from appealing the claim decision.

### Question 43: APPEAL-18

**Query:** What if my appeal is denied?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 1.92s

**Answer:**

If your appeal is denied, you have a couple of options:

- If you disagree with the Board’s decision and have new and relevant evidence that supports your case, you can file a Supplemental Claim.
- Alternatively, you can appeal to the U.S. Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims.

These options allow you to continue pursuing your claim despite the denial¹.

### Question 45: APPEAL-20

**Query:** What is duty to assist error?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 2.13s

**Answer:**

A duty to assist error occurs when the VA fails to fulfill its obligation to help Veterans obtain the evidence needed to support their claim or appeal. One common example of a duty to assist error is the failure to send the foundational notice known as the "5103 notice," which explains what evidence is required to properly support a claim or appeal¹.

### Question 46: RATING-1

**Query:** How are VA disability ratings calculated?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 5.44s

**Answer:**

VA disability ratings are calculated based on the severity of a Veteran's service-connected condition(s) as determined by the Veterans Benefits Administration (VBA) using the Schedule for Rating Disabilities in conjunction with the M21-1 Adjudication Procedures Manual. The rating percentage reflects how much the disability impairs the Veteran's ability to function and is intended to compensate for the average impairment in earning capacity caused by the disability.

For mental health conditions, the rating is determined by evaluating the totality of symptoms and evidence submitted, including C&P examiner reports, Disability Benefits Questionnaires (DBQs), nexus letters, buddy letters, personal statements, treatment notes, and other relevant documentation. The rater assigns a rating percentage that most closely matches the overall severity of the Veteran's symptoms, not necessarily requiring all symptoms listed for a particular rating level to be present¹³.

Each condition is assigned a diagnostic code (DC) that corresponds to specific rating criteria. For example, PTSD is under DC 9411, hypertension under DC 7101, and tinnitus under DC 6260¹.

The combined disability rating is calculated when a Veteran has multiple service-connected disabilities, and this combined rating determines eligibility for various benefits such as Individual Unemployability, nursing home care, and other programs. For example, a combined rating of 70% or more may qualify a Veteran for Individual Unemployability and certain health care benefits².

It is important not to misuse or misrepresent the severity of a condition on the rating schedule, as this is considered a crime. The rating schedule should be used as a guide to understand how the VA evaluates disabilities and what evidence is relevant to support a claim⁴⁵.

**Sources:**
¹ [Common VA Diagnostic Codes Reference](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)  
² [70%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/combinedbenefits)  
³ [How a Rater Decides](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
⁴ [The Rating Schedule Index](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)  
⁵ [The Rating Schedule Index](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Common VA Diagnostic Codes Reference](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)

### Question 47: RATING-2

**Query:** What is combined rating?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 2.66s

**Answer:**

A combined rating refers to the total disability rating a Veteran receives when multiple service-connected conditions are considered together. The combined rating is used to determine the overall level of disability and the corresponding benefits. Importantly, a Veteran who is rated at a certain combined percentage (for example, 50%) receives all the benefits associated with that rating as well as all benefits from lower combined ratings. This means benefits accumulate as the combined rating increases¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Federal Benefits List by Combined Ratings - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/combinedbenefits)

### Question 49: RATING-4

**Query:** What is the bilateral factor?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 5.44s

**Answer:**

The bilateral factor is an additional 10% added to the VA disability rating calculation when a Veteran has a rated condition (more than 0%) that affects both the left and right sides of the same extremity half—either the upper extremity (above the waist) or the lower extremity (below the waist). This 10% is added after combining the bilateral conditions' ratings and then factored back into the overall disability rating calculation¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Bilateral Factor - (rating_calculation)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#bilateral#bilateralfactor)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Bilateral Factor](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#bilateral#bilateralfactor)

### Question 50: RATING-5

**Query:** Can I get 100% disability?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 4.25s

**Answer:**

You may be eligible for a temporary 100% disability rating if you are hospitalized or convalescing due to a service-connected condition. To qualify, you must provide medical evidence that clearly states:

- The condition(s) causing the hospitalization or convalescence
- The length of the hospitalization or convalescence stay

If related to surgery, the evidence must also include:

- Date of admission and discharge
- Date of surgery
- Length of convalescence (if not dictated by the rating schedule)
- Procedure performed

If you are hospitalized at a VA facility, the VA should handle the claim for you, and you may not need to file yourself. You can file a new claim for "temporary 100% due to hospitalization" or "temporary 100% due to convalescence" online or with the help of a Veterans Service Organization (VSO)³⁶⁷.

For a permanent 100% disability rating, the context does not provide specific criteria or information. However, temporary 100% ratings are granted primarily during hospitalization or convalescence related to service-connected conditions.

### Question 51: RATING-6

**Query:** What is the difference between schedular and TDIU?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 4.18s

**Answer:**

The difference between schedular and TDIU (Total Disability Individual Unemployability) lies in how the VA rates a veteran's disability and the impact on their ability to work:

- **Schedular Rating:** This is the disability rating assigned based on the severity of the veteran's service-connected conditions according to the VA's Schedule for Rating Disabilities. It reflects the level of impairment but does not necessarily consider the veteran's ability to maintain gainful employment¹.

- **TDIU (Total Disability based on Individual Unemployability):** This is a special VA benefit that allows veterans who are unable to hold down substantially gainful employment due to their service-connected disabilities to be compensated at the 100% disability rate, even if their combined schedular rating is less than 100%. To qualify for TDIU, a veteran must have at least one service-connected disability rated at 60% or higher and be unable to maintain steady employment because of their disabilities. Marginal or odd jobs do not disqualify a veteran from TDIU². TDIU is meant to compensate for the economic impact of the disability on employability rather than just the medical severity.

In summary, schedular ratings are based on medical severity, while TDIU considers the veteran's inability to work due to service-connected disabilities and provides 100% compensation accordingly.

**Sources:**
¹ [Unemployability - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)  
² [50% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Unemployability](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)
- [50%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)
- [Veterans Readiness and Employment (VR&E) Chapter 31 - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vre#faq)
- [100% P&T or TDIU P&T](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)
- [The Rating Schedule Index](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)
- [The Rating Schedule Index](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)
- [100% or TDIU](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)

### Question 53: RATING-8

**Query:** What is SMC (Special Monthly Compensation)?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 3.36s

**Answer:**

Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) is a higher level of monthly tax-free monetary benefit provided by the VA for veterans who have specific needs or disabilities that require additional aid and attendance or result in housebound status due to service-related conditions. It can provide benefits ranging from over $100 to over $10,000 monthly. For example, veterans who need regular aid and attendance due to a service-connected condition such as traumatic brain injury (TBI) may qualify for SMC. Specific types of SMC include SMC-S for housebound veterans and SMC-L or SMC-R1/R2 for those needing aid and attendance due to service-related disabilities³⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/TBI)

³ [Aid and Attendance (Pension) - (pension)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-aa#aidandattendancepension)  
⁴ [VA Disability - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/smc)

### Question 55: RATING-10

**Query:** What is housebound status?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 2.69s

**Answer:**

Housebound status refers to a condition where a veteran is substantially confined to their immediate premises due to a disability. Veterans who are housebound as a result of non-service-connected conditions may qualify for an increased VA Pension benefit. If the housebound status is due to service-connected conditions, the veteran may be eligible for Special Monthly Compensation (SMC), specifically the SMC-S (Housebound) benefit¹.

### Question 56: RATING-11

**Query:** What is aid and attendance?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 2.69s

**Answer:**

Aid and Attendance is a benefit that provides an increased VA Pension for veterans who need help with daily living activities due to their medical conditions. ### Question 57: RATING-12

**Query:** Can my rating be reduced?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 5.46s

**Answer:**

Yes, your VA disability rating can be reduced under certain circumstances:

- If you file an appeal, the VA will review your file. If they find a Clear and Unmistakable Error (CUE) where you were rated too high or service-connection was granted in error, they will take action to correct it, which can include reducing your rating¹.
- If exams ordered as part of your claim show that your condition has improved, the VA can start action to reduce your rating¹.
- However, a single Compensation & Pension (C&P) exam showing improvement is generally not enough to reduce a rating if the evaluation has been held for at least 5 continuous years. Continuous improvement must be shown, usually requiring more than one exam showing improvement before a proposal for reduction is issued. There are exceptions, such as conditions rated based on lab results (e.g., kidney or lung function), where a single exam can trigger a reduction if it confirms improvement and you filed for an increase on that condition²³.
- The 5-year protection against reduction does not reset, meaning the VA can still reduce your rating based on improvement even after many years if evidence supports it²³.

In summary, your rating can be reduced if there is a clear error or if your condition improves continuously, but there are protections in place to prevent reductions based on a single exam if your rating has been stable for 5 years or more.

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)  
² [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344) - references](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)  
³ [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344) - references](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)

**Sources Cited:**

### Question 58: RATING-13

**Query:** What is a protected rating?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 3.31s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **48.3%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

A protected rating refers to a VA disability rating that cannot be reduced for a certain period of time once it has been held continuously. According to the example involving rating increases:

- A rating that has been held for 5 years is protected from reduction for that 5-year period.
- A rating held for 10 years also gains protection.
- A rating held for 20 years is protected as well.

However, if a rating is increased, the protection applies only to the original rating percentage that has been held for the required time, not to the newly increased rating. For example, if a condition was rated 10% for 10 years and then increased to 30%, the 10% rating has 20-year protection, but the 30% rating does not immediately gain the same protection until it is held for the required time period.

**Sources:**
¹ [Example Involving Increase - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)

### Question 59: RATING-14

**Query:** What is static vs dynamic rating?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 3.34s

**Answer:**

A static rating is a VA disability rating for a service-connected condition that is not subject to regular future evaluations or reexaminations. This means the condition is considered stable and unlikely to improve or worsen, so the rating remains fixed unless there is a clear error or new evidence. Static conditions are not expected to be reevaluated by the VA over time.

In contrast, a dynamic rating applies to conditions that may change over time and are subject to periodic reevaluation. These conditions can improve or worsen, so the VA may schedule future exams to adjust the disability rating accordingly.

You can find which of your conditions are static or dynamic through online tools or by requesting your C-File, which includes a code sheet listing all conditions and their static status¹³⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Static vs Dynamic Ratings - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/static-check#faq)  
³ [Static vs Dynamic Ratings - (rating_info)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/static-check#staticvsdynamicratings)  
⁴ [Static vs Dynamic Ratings - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/static-check#faq)

**Sources Cited:**

### Question 61: RATING-16

**Query:** What is an effective date?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 2.68s

**Answer:**

An effective date is the date from which a VA benefit or compensation is considered to begin. For 1151 claims, the effective date can be either:

- The date of the injury or aggravation, if the claim is received within one year of the incident; or
- The date the claim was initiated.

For Dependency and Indemnity Compensation (DIC), the effective date can be either:

- The first day of the month in which the Veteran’s death occurred, if a claim is received within one year following the date of death; or
- The date the claim was received.

### Question 64: RATING-19

**Query:** What is P&T (Permanent and Total)?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 3.94s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **47.2%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

⚠️ **Note:** Some specific numbers in this response could not be verified against my sources. Please verify key statistics with official VA documentation.

P&T (Permanent and Total) refers to a disability rating status where a veteran is rated 100% disabled permanently and totally. This status provides several benefits including:

- Entitlement to Chapter 35 Dependents Educational Assistance (DEA).
- Removal of the 10% penalty for early withdrawal of 401K and IRAs.
- Eligibility for the veteran's dependents to enroll in CHAMPVA (Civilian Health and Medical Program of the Department of Veterans Affairs).
- During a medical emergency, the veteran may freely use non-VA emergency rooms for any condition and must submit a claim within 2 years for reimbursement.
- Expedited processing of the veteran's disability claims through Social Security.
- Free air travel on Space-A, which can be used by the veteran's dependents if the veteran accompanies them. This travel includes flights within the Continental United States and between CONUS and certain U.S. territories and states.

These benefits highlight the comprehensive support provided to veterans with a Permanent and Total disability rating¹.

### Question 65: RATING-20

**Query:** What is the 5 year rule?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 3.81s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **47.4%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

The 5 year rule refers to the protection a Veteran has against rating reductions based on a single Compensation & Pension (C&P) exam showing improvement. Specifically:

- A Veteran's rating generally cannot be reduced based on just one exam that shows the disability has improved.
- Continuous improvement must be demonstrated over a period of at least 5 continuous years before a reduction can be proposed.
- In most cases, the VA will require another exam confirming the continued improvement before issuing a proposal for reduction if the reduction affects the combined evaluation.
- If the reduction does not affect the combined evaluation, the condition may be reduced without notice.
- This protection does not reset after 5 years. For example, if an exam shows improvement at year 6 and another at year 16, the VA can still pursue a reduction based on these findings.
- There are exceptions for conditions rated based on lab results or those with temporary or episodic improvement.

In summary, the 5 year rule means that a Veteran's disability rating is protected from reduction unless continuous improvement is shown over at least 5 years.

**Sources:**
¹ [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)
² [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)

**Sources Cited:**
- [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)
- [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)

### Question 66: RATING-21

**Query:** What is the 10 year rule?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 4.04s

**Answer:**

The 10 year rule refers to the protection of a Veteran's service-connection for a disability after 10 years from the date of the original grant. Specifically:

- A Veteran's service-connection for a disability **cannot** be severed after 10 years, except in cases of fraud or if military records clearly show the Veteran did not have the required service or character of discharge.
- Erroneously service-connected conditions remain eligible for increases if warranted.
- Disabilities caused by an erroneously service-connected condition can be service-connected on a secondary basis.
- The VA can reassign the location of a service-connected disability (e.g., left vs. right side) even after 10 years, but if this results in a reduced evaluation, the VA must provide due process and propose the change.
- Periods during which a Veteran returns to Active Duty do not count toward the 10-year calculation.

This rule is codified in 38 CFR 3.957.

**Sources:**
¹ [10 Years - Service-Connection (38 CFR 3.957) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)  
² [10 Years - Service-Connection (38 CFR 3.957) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

**Sources Cited:**
- [10 Years - Service-Connection (38 CFR 3.957)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)
- [10 Years - Service-Connection (38 CFR 3.957)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

### Question 67: RATING-22

**Query:** What is the 20 year rule?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 3.59s

**Answer:**

The 20 year rule, as described in 38 CFR 3.951, means that a Veteran's entitlement to a benefit or rating cannot be severed or reduced after 20 years, except in cases of fraud. However, there are some special notes to this rule:

- If the VA clearly and erroneously granted service connection for a disability involving a specific location (such as left vs. right or upper vs. lower), the VA can reassign the location of the grant even after 20 years. If this reassignment results in a reduced evaluation, the VA must provide due process and propose the change.
- Periods during which a Veteran is returned to Active Duty do not count toward calculating this 20-year protection.
- The pausing of Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) based on the need for aid and attendance is allowed when the Veteran is hospitalized at government expense.

**Sources:**
¹ [20 Years - Total Protection (38 CFR 3.951) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)

### Question 68: RATING-23

### Question 70: RATING-25

**Query:** How do I apply for TDIU?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 3.49s

**Answer:**

To apply for Total Disability based on Individual Unemployability (TDIU), you should:

- Submit an application for TDIU through the VA. While the exact form for TDIU is not specified in the provided context, generally, applying for VA disability benefits involves filling out the appropriate VA forms.
- If you are applying for related benefits such as Dependency and Indemnity Compensation (DIC), Death Pension, or Accrued Benefits, you would fill out VA Form 21-534EZ, but this form is not specifically for TDIU¹.
- Since TDIU is for veterans who are unable to hold down substantially gainful employment due to service-connected disabilities, you should provide evidence of your service-connected disabilities and how they prevent you from maintaining gainful employment¹ ⁶.

For detailed instructions and the specific forms to apply for TDIU, it is recommended to visit the VA website or consult a Veterans Service Organization.

### Question 71: MENTAL-1

**Query:** How is PTSD rated by the VA?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 5.89s

**Answer:**

PTSD (Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder) is rated by the VA as a mental health disorder based on the severity of the totality of the Veteran's symptoms, not on the specific diagnosis itself. Veterans receive a single mental health rating regardless of the number of diagnoses they have, including PTSD¹ ⁷.

PTSD does have unique service-connection criteria requiring an in-service stressor, which can include various traumatic events such as combat exposure, assault, military sexual trauma, accidents, and others³. However, once service-connected, PTSD is rated using the same general mental health rating criteria as other mental disorders¹.

The rating percentage is determined by how closely the Veteran's symptoms match the criteria for each rating level. For example, a 50% rating corresponds to occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity due to symptoms such as panic attacks more than once a week, difficulty understanding complex commands, impaired judgment, disturbances of mood, and difficulty maintaining effective work and social relationships².

Additionally, PTSD and TBI (Traumatic Brain Injury) symptoms often overlap and may be "lumped" together for rating purposes, but if the symptoms can be clearly separated by a specialist, separate ratings for each condition can be assigned⁴.

In summary:

- PTSD is rated as a mental disorder based on symptom severity, not diagnosis alone¹ ⁷.
- Service-connection for PTSD requires an in-service stressor³.
- Ratings reflect the overall impact of symptoms on occupational and social functioning, with specific symptom examples defining rating levels such as 50%².
- PTSD and TBI may be rated together or separately depending on symptom differentiation⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Mental Disorder Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
² [50% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
³ [PTSD Service-Connection](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
⁴ [PTSD & TBI - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
⁷ [Mental Disorders - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

**Sources Cited:**

### Question 72: MENTAL-2

**Query:** What symptoms qualify for PTSD rating?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 4.96s

**Answer:**

The symptoms that qualify for a PTSD rating include those that cause occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity. Specifically, for a 50% rating, symptoms may include:

- Flattened affect
- Circumstantial, circumlocutory, or stereotyped speech
- Panic attacks more than once a week
- Difficulty in understanding complex commands
- Impairment of short- and long-term memory (e.g., retention of only highly learned material, forgetting to complete tasks)
- Impaired judgment
- Impaired abstract thinking
- Disturbances of motivation and mood
- Difficulty in establishing and maintaining effective work and social relationships

Additional details on functioning include:

- Mostly able to take care of themselves, though hygiene may not always be maintained
- Able to do daily tasks such as driving, shopping, household chores, etc.
- May occasionally be hospitalized
- Can work but may find it difficult to stay employed for long periods
- May form relationships, but these may be short-lived due to inability to be an active participant
- Extreme lack of motivation and/or mood swings
- Difficulty expressing themselves or showing emotions, possibly responding with inappropriate emotions
- Constant signs of severe anxiety
- Difficulty thinking logically and making poor judgments, possibly requiring a fiduciary

These symptoms reflect the severity of the PTSD for rating purposes, which is based on the totality of symptoms rather than the diagnosis itself.

**Sources:**

¹ [PTSD Service-Connection - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
² [50% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 73: MENTAL-3

**Query:** Can I get service connected for PTSD without combat?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 3.38s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get service-connected for PTSD without having been in direct combat. PTSD service-connection requires an in-service stressor, but this stressor does not have to be combat-related. Examples of non-combat in-service stressors that can qualify include:

- Aircraft crashes  
- Assault or battery  
- Environmental disasters (earthquakes, fires, floods, etc.)  
- Harassment or military sexual trauma (MST) such as sexual assault or harassment  
- Serving as a drone aircraft crew member  
- Serving in mortuary affairs  
- Stalking  
- Training accidents  
- Vehicular crashes  

Fear of hostile enemy forces is also a qualifying stressor, but it does not include anticipation of future deployment or learning about the death of someone away from the veteran in a hostile area¹.

Therefore, combat is not a requirement to establish service connection for PTSD as long as there is a verified in-service stressor of the types listed above.

**Sources:**

**Category:** MENTAL

**Answer:**

### Question 77: MENTAL-7

**Query:** What is the 100% rating for mental health?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 4.16s

**Answer:**

The 100% rating for mental health indicates total occupational and social impairment due to symptoms such as:

- Gross impairment in thought processes or communication
- Persistent delusions or hallucinations
- Grossly inappropriate behavior
- Persistent danger of hurting self or others
- Intermittent inability to perform activities of daily living (including maintenance of minimal personal hygiene)
- Disorientation to time or place
- Memory loss for names of close relatives, own occupation, or own name

Additional details include:

- The Veteran is unable to care for themselves and may require constant or near-constant hospitalization due to being a threat to themselves or others.
- There is no ability to work, except possibly in a protected environment.
- Social relationships, if they exist, are one-way and directed toward the Veteran; the Veteran does not seek or encourage social interaction.
- Symptoms may include constant risk to self or others (including suicidal tendencies), significant memory loss, constant severe anxiety, fear, paranoia, inappropriate behavior (e.g., drooling, mumbling, shouting), inability to differentiate reality from make-believe, inability to understand time or location, and inability to communicate, reason, or think logically¹.

### Question 78: MENTAL-8

**Query:** Can I have multiple mental health ratings?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 1.96s

**Answer:**

No, you cannot have multiple mental health ratings. Veterans can only have one mental health rating that combines the symptoms of all their mental disorders. The only exception is if the Veteran has an eating disorder; in that case, they will receive one rating for their mental health disorders and a separate rating for the eating disorder¹.

### Question 79: MENTAL-9

**Query:** How is anxiety rated?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 5.81s

**Answer:**

Anxiety is rated based on the severity of the totality of a Veteran's symptoms rather than the specific diagnosis. Veterans receive a single rating regardless of the number of mental health diagnoses they have. The rating depends on how symptoms affect occupational and social functioning, work efficiency, and daily life.

Here are some example rating levels for anxiety symptoms:

- **10% rating:** Occupational and social impairment due to mild or transient symptoms that decrease work efficiency only during significant stress or symptoms controlled by continuous medication. Symptoms include mild anxiety, occasional mild panic attacks, and occasional sleep disturbances. The Veteran is always able to care for themselves and rarely has work problems¹³.

- **30% rating:** Occupational and social impairment with occasional decrease in work efficiency and intermittent inability to perform occupational tasks. Symptoms include depressed mood, anxiety, suspiciousness, weekly or less frequent panic attacks, chronic sleep impairment, and mild memory loss. The Veteran can usually care for themselves but may occasionally be hospitalized. Social relationships may be strained¹⁵.

- **50% rating:** Occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity. Symptoms include flattened affect, frequent panic attacks (more than once a week), difficulty understanding complex commands, impaired memory and judgment, disturbances of motivation and mood, and difficulty maintaining relationships. The Veteran is mostly able to care for themselves but may have hygiene issues and difficulty maintaining employment long-term¹⁶.

The Veteran's formal diagnosis (e.g., generalized anxiety disorder, panic disorder, PTSD) does not affect the rating level; the rating is based on symptom severity and functional impairment¹⁴.

**Sources:**

**Sources Cited:**
- [Anxiety Disorders](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [The Nervous System Index](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [10%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [Mental Disorder Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [30%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [50%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 80: MENTAL-10

**Query:** How is depression rated?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 3.68s

**Answer:**

Depression is rated as a mental disorder based on the severity of the totality of the Veteran's symptoms rather than the formal diagnosis itself. The rating is determined by how severe the symptoms are, regardless of whether the Veteran has depression, anxiety, PTSD, or multiple diagnoses. The Veteran receives a single rating based on symptom severity, not the number of diagnoses¹.

For example, a 50% rating corresponds to occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity due to symptoms such as:

- Flattened affect  
- Panic attacks more than once a week  
- Difficulty understanding complex commands  
- Impairment of short- and long-term memory  
- Impaired judgment and abstract thinking  
- Disturbances of motivation and mood  
- Difficulty establishing and maintaining effective work and social relationships  

At this level, the Veteran is mostly able to care for themselves but may have difficulty maintaining hygiene consistently and may occasionally be hospitalized. They can work but may have trouble staying employed long-term and may form relationships that are short-lived due to their symptoms².

Ultimately, the rating decision is based on the totality of symptoms and all evidence submitted, including medical exams, treatment notes, and personal statements³.
