### Question 247: LEJEUNE-7

**Query:** What is the Camp Lejeune Justice Act?

**Category:** LEJEUNE

**Response Time:** 3.86s

**Answer:**

The Camp Lejeune Justice Act is a law included in Section 804 of the Honoring our Promise to Address Comprehensive Toxics (PACT) Act. It allows Veterans, survivors, and families to file for appropriate relief for harm caused by exposure to contaminated water at Camp Lejeune. To be eligible to file for relief under this law, you (or your legal representative) must meet one of the following conditions:

- You lived, worked, or were otherwise exposed at Camp Lejeune for at least 30 days total between August 1, 1953, and December 31, 1987, **or**
- Your mother lived, worked, or was otherwise exposed at Camp Lejeune for at least 30 days total between August 1, 1953, and December 31, 1987, while pregnant with you

The law provides a pathway to file an administrative claim with the Department of the Navy for relief related to this exposure. It is important to note that the PACT Act, which includes the Camp Lejeune Justice Act, does not affect VA benefits related to Camp Lejeune but expands VA health care and benefits for Veterans exposed to other toxic substances like burn pits¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [How the PACT Act affects Camp Lejeune claims and related benefits](https://www.va.gov/disability/eligibility/hazardous-materials-exposure/camp-lejeune-water-contamination/#howthepactactaffectscamplejeuneclaimsandrelatedbenefits)

**Sources Cited:**
- [How the PACT Act affects Camp Lejeune claims and related benefits](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)

- [On this page](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [Benefits for families who lived at Camp Lejeune](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)

### Question 249: YELLOW-1

**Query:** What is the Yellow Ribbon program?

**Category:** YELLOW

**Response Time:** 4.05s

**Answer:**

The Yellow Ribbon Program is a provision related to the Post-9/11 GI Bill that helps cover tuition and fee costs that exceed the standard benefits. The program involves an agreement between the VA and participating schools to share the cost of tuition and fees that are not covered by the GI Bill. The specific amount and availability depend on the school and factors such as degree type and program. To participate, veterans must apply for Post-9/11 GI Bill benefits, submit their certificate of eligibility to their school, and request to apply for the Yellow Ribbon Program. The school then determines the number of students accepted and the amount of funding available, notifying the student of approval and the amount awarded³⁴⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Reference - (education)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#reference)  
² [Yellow Ribbon Program - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#faq)  
³ [Yellow Ribbon Program - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#faq)  
⁴ [Yellow Ribbon Program - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#faq)  
⁶ [Application Process - (education)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#applicationprocess)

### Question 251: YELLOW-3

**Query:** How does Yellow Ribbon work with GI Bill?

**Category:** YELLOW

**Response Time:** 5.04s

**Answer:**

The Yellow Ribbon Program is specifically associated with the Post-9/11 GI Bill (Chapter 33) and not with the Montgomery GI Bill (Chapter 30). Here is how it works with the Post-9/11 GI Bill:

- The Post-9/11 GI Bill provides up to 36 months of education benefits, including full tuition and mandatory fees paid directly to public institutions.
- For private or foreign schools, tuition and fees are capped at a national maximum rate.
- Veterans attending private higher learning institutions may be eligible for the Yellow Ribbon Program, which helps cover additional education-related costs not covered by the VA.
- To use the Yellow Ribbon Program, you must apply for it through your school’s certifying official or financial aid office when submitting your certificate of eligibility (COE).
- The school will determine if it has reached the maximum number of students accepted into the Yellow Ribbon Program and the amount of money you will receive.
- You will be notified by your school if you are approved for the Yellow Ribbon Program and the amount awarded.

The Montgomery GI Bill (Chapter 30) does not mention eligibility or use of the Yellow Ribbon Program in the provided context.

Therefore, Yellow Ribbon is a benefit enhancement available only for those using the Post-9/11 GI Bill and attending private or foreign schools to help cover tuition and fees beyond the VA cap.

**Sources:**
¹ [Application Process - (education)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#applicationprocess)  
² [Chapter 33 (Post-9/11 GI Bill) (Part 3) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/gibills#chapter33post911gibi)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Application Process](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#applicationprocess)
- [Chapter 33 (Post-9/11 GI Bill) (Part 3)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/gibills#chapter33post911gibi)
- [Chapter 33 (Post-9/11 GI Bill) (Part 1)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/gibills#chapter33post911gibi)
- [GI Bill Education Benefits - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/gibill#faq)

- [Chapter 30 (Montgomery GI Bill (MGIB) - Active Duty)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/gibill#chapter30montgomerygibillmgibactiveduty)

### Question 254: YELLOW-6

**Query:** How do I apply for Yellow Ribbon?

**Category:** YELLOW

**Response Time:** 2.2s

**Answer:**

To apply for the Yellow Ribbon Program, follow these steps:

1. Apply for Post-9/11 GI Bill benefits through the VA website.
2. Submit your Certificate of Eligibility (COE) to your school’s certifying official (SCO), financial aid office, or military/Veteran liaison. When you submit your COE, be sure to ask to apply for your school’s Yellow Ribbon Program.
3. Wait for your school to determine if they have reached the maximum number of students accepted into the program and the amount of money you will receive.
4. Your school will notify you if you have been approved and the amount of funding you will get from the program¹.

### Question 255: PENSION-1

**Query:** What is Aid and Attendance?

**Category:** PENSION

**Response Time:** 1.8s

**Answer:**

Aid and Attendance is a benefit that provides an increased VA Pension for veterans who need assistance with daily living activities. This need for aid and attendance can be due to non-service related conditions, in which case the veteran may qualify for an increased pension benefit. If the need for aid and attendance is due to service-related conditions, the veteran may be eligible for Special Monthly Compensation (SMC), specifically SMC L (Aid and Attendance) or SMC R1/R2 (Special Aid and Attendance)¹.

**Sources:**

### Question 256: PENSION-2

**Query:** Am I eligible for Aid and Attendance?

**Category:** PENSION

**Response Time:** 4.71s

**Answer:**

To be eligible for Aid and Attendance (A&A), you must:

- Qualify for VA Pension; **AND**
- Meet at least one of the following criteria:
  - Need another person to help you perform daily activities such as bathing, feeding, and dressing;
  - Have to stay in bed or spend a large portion of the day in bed due to illness;
  - Are a patient in a nursing home because of loss of mental or physical abilities related to a disability;
  - Have limited eyesight (even with glasses or contacts, 5/200 or less in both eyes, or concentric contraction of the visual field to 5 degrees or less)¹.

If your need for aid and attendance is due to a non-service connected condition, you may qualify for an increased VA Pension. If it is due to a service-connected condition, you should look into Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) benefits such as SMC L or SMC R1/R2².

**Sources:**
¹ [Eligibility - Aid and Attendance (Pension)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-aa#eligibility)  
² [Aid and Attendance (Pension)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-aa#aidandattendancepension)

### Question 257: PENSION-3

**Query:** What is housebound status?

**Category:** PENSION

**Response Time:** 1.88s

**Answer:**

Housebound status refers to a condition where a veteran is substantially confined to their immediate premises due to a disability. Veterans who are housebound as a result of non-service related conditions may qualify for an increased VA Pension benefit. For veterans who are housebound due to service-related conditions, they may be eligible for Special Monthly Compensation (SMC), specifically the SMC-S (Housebound) benefit¹.

### Question 258: PENSION-4

**Query:** What is Special Monthly Pension?

**Category:** PENSION

**Response Time:** 2.9s

**Answer:**

Special Monthly Pension (SMP) is an increased VA pension benefit available to veterans who are housebound or need aid and attendance due to non-service related conditions. Veterans who are housebound as a result of service-related conditions may qualify for Special Monthly Compensation (SMC), which includes specific categories such as SMC-S for housebound status and SMC L or SMC R1/R2 for aid and attendance needs. SMP provides additional financial support beyond the basic pension for veterans with severe disabilities that limit their ability to leave their home or require regular aid and attendance¹⁴.

### Question 259: PENSION-5

**Query:** How do I apply for Aid and Attendance?

**Category:** PENSION

**Response Time:** 7.0s

**Answer:**

To apply for Aid and Attendance (A&A), you should:

- Visit the Aid and Attendance application information page by clicking [HERE](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/aah) for detailed instructions on applying¹ ⁶.

- Generally, you will need to fill out the appropriate application forms, such as the Application for DIC, Death Pension, and/or Accrued Benefits (VA Form 21-534EZ) if applying as a surviving spouse or for pension benefits related to Aid and Attendance² ³.

- Submit your application and supporting documents either online, by fax, or by mail. Supporting documents may include medical evidence of the need for Aid and Attendance, proof of Medicare status if applicable, and other personal documents like birth certificates or marriage certificates to expedite processing⁵ ⁷.

- For veterans needing Aid and Attendance due to non-service-related conditions, this applies to increased VA Pension benefits. For service-related conditions, Aid and Attendance benefits are part of Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) and require a different application process².

For the most accurate and detailed application instructions, refer to the linked Aid and Attendance application page.

**Sources:**
¹ [Applying - (pension)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-aa#applying)  
² [Aid and Attendance (Pension)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-aa#aidandattendancepension)

### Question 261: PENSION-7

### Question 262: PENSION-8

**Query:** What is VA pension?

**Category:** PENSION

**Response Time:** 2.86s

**Answer:**

VA Pension is a benefit provided by the Department of Veterans Affairs that offers monthly payments to qualifying veterans. To be eligible for VA Pension, a veteran must qualify for the pension based on income and net worth limits and typically must have a permanent disability that confines them to spending most of their time at home¹. Additionally, there are enhanced pension benefits such as Aid and Attendance for veterans who need help with daily activities or are bedridden due to illness, or Housebound benefits for those who are substantially confined to their home due to disability⁴⁶.

### Question 263: DTA-1

**Query:** What is VA's duty to assist?

**Category:** DTA

**Response Time:** 5.4s

**Answer:**

The VA's duty to assist is a legal obligation to help Veterans obtain the benefits and recognition they deserve by assisting in two main areas:

- Obtaining records relevant to the claim.
- Providing a medical examination or obtaining medical opinions when necessary.

A foundational part of this duty is the VA's duty to notify, which involves sending a "5103 notice" that explains what evidence is needed to properly support a claim or appeal. Failure to send this notice is a common duty to assist error.

Additionally, the VA should assist claimants even if the claim is vague or incomplete by clarifying what benefits are being claimed or informing the claimant if the application is incomplete.

For the VA to have a duty to provide a medical examination or obtain medical opinions, three elements must all be met:

1. A statement of symptoms or current diagnosis.
2. Evidence of an in-service event or a service-connected disability that caused or worsened the condition.
3. A reasonable possibility that the condition and the in-service event or service-connected disability are related (nexus).

If these criteria are not met, the VA has no duty to provide an examination or medical opinion.

Also, there is no duty to assist with a new exam or opinion for supplemental claims if the Veteran does not submit new and relevant evidence with their appeal. Furthermore, if the rater can grant the maximum schedular evaluation based on existing evidence, an examination is not needed.

In summary, the VA's duty to assist includes helping Veterans by obtaining necessary records, providing medical examinations or opinions when appropriate, and notifying Veterans of what evidence is required to support their claims³⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Duty to Assist (DTA) - (claims_process)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#dutytoassistdta)  
² [Standard Procedures - (claims_process)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#standardprocedures)  
³ [Duty to Assist (DTA) - Legal References - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#references)  
⁶ [Providing a Medical Examination/Obtaining Medical Opinions - (claims_process)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#providingamedicalexaminationobtainingmedicalopinions)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Duty to Assist (DTA)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#dutytoassistdta)

### Question 264: DTA-2

**Query:** What is VA's duty to notify?

**Category:** DTA

**Response Time:** 2.82s

**Answer:**

The VA's duty to notify is a foundational part of the VA's duty to assist veterans in their claims process. It requires the VA to send a "5103 notice" to the claimant, which explains what evidence is needed to properly support their claim or appeal. This notice informs the veteran about the information and evidence required to substantiate their claim. Failure to send this notice is a common error related to the duty to assist. The duty to notify ensures that veterans are properly informed about the requirements for their claims, helping them to provide the necessary documentation and evidence for a fair evaluation¹³.

### Question 265: DTA-3

**Query:** Does the VA have to get my records?

**Category:** DTA

**Response Time:** 5.27s

**Answer:**

The VA has a duty to make reasonable efforts to obtain relevant records related to your claimed disabilities that will reasonably further your claim. This usually involves about two attempts to get these records, which can include military, federal, private medical, employment, and other records. However, the VA will not pay any fees to obtain records, and if you fail to provide enough detail or complete a release form (such as VA Form 21-4142) within 30 days, the VA may decide your claim without further attempts to get those records.

While the VA will try to get your Service Treatment Records (STRs) and private medical records for you, you must provide proper authorization (like VA Form 21-4142) for private records. Ultimately, it is your responsibility to supply these records if the VA cannot obtain them. If the VA cannot get certain records, they must notify you about what records they could not get, the attempts made, and any further actions they will take.

In summary:

- VA will make reasonable efforts to get relevant records related to your claim.
- You must provide necessary details and authorizations for the VA to obtain private medical records.
- VA will not pay fees for records.
- If you do not respond timely or provide releases, VA may decide without those records.
- It is ultimately your responsibility to provide records if VA cannot obtain them.
- VA must notify you if they cannot get certain records and explain their efforts.

Therefore, yes, the VA does have to try to get your records but within reasonable limits and with your cooperation³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Obtaining Records - (claims_process)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#obtainingrecords)  
² [Service Treatment Records (STRs)/Military Medical Records - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/infect)  
³ [Obtaining/Types of Evidence - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

### Question 266: DTA-4

**Query:** Can I complain if VA didn't help get my records?

**Category:** DTA

**Response Time:** 3.09s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can raise a complaint or at least expect the VA to notify you if they were unable to obtain your records after making reasonable efforts. The VA is required to make reasonable efforts (usually two attempts) to obtain relevant records related to your claimed disabilities. However, if they cannot obtain the records, they must notify you about:

- What records they could not get;
- The attempts made to obtain those records;
- That after continued efforts, they are reasonably certain the records do not exist or further efforts would be futile;
- Any further actions the VA will take regarding your claim.

Ultimately, it is the Veteran's responsibility to supply records if the VA cannot obtain them. If the VA fails to notify you properly or does not make reasonable efforts, you may have grounds to complain or seek assistance. Also, note that if you do not respond timely (within 30 days) to requests for more information or releases, the VA may decide your claim without further attempts to get those records¹.

### Question 267: DTA-5

**Query:** What records will the VA request for me?

**Category:** DTA

**Response Time:** 4.33s

**Answer:**

The VA will make reasonable efforts to obtain relevant records that relate to your claimed disabilities and will reasonably further your claim. These records commonly fall into five categories:

- Military records (medical and personnel)
- Federal (non-military) records
- Private medical records
- Employment (non-federal) records
- Other relevant records

The VA will attempt to obtain these records, usually making two attempts, but will not pay any fees to obtain them. If you do not respond timely to requests for information or releases, the VA may decide your claim without further attempts to get those records. Ultimately, it is your responsibility to supply records that the VA cannot obtain after reasonable efforts¹.

For private medical records and Vet Center records, the VA requires you to provide a signed VA Form 21-4142/21-4142a (records release) to request those records on your behalf⁴.

The VA can generally access your VA medical records themselves, so you do not need to submit those separately³⁴.

If the VA cannot obtain certain records, they will notify you about which records could not be obtained, the attempts made, and any further actions they will take¹.

### Question 268: DTA-6

**Response Time:** 1.88s

**Answer:**

A duty to assist error occurs when the VA fails to fulfill its obligation to help Veterans obtain the benefits they deserve, particularly when the VA does not provide the foundational notice known as a "5103 notice." ### Question 269: EDGE-1

**Query:** Can I get VA benefits with a bad conduct discharge?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 3.72s

**Answer:**

You may still be eligible for VA benefits with a bad conduct discharge, depending on the specifics of your discharge characterization. The basic eligibility for VA disability benefits requires that your character of discharge be Honorable, General, or Under Honorable. However, even if your character of discharge was not Honorable, you may still be eligible¹.

On the other hand, if your discharge was dishonorable, you generally will not be eligible. Also, certain convictions, such as federal or state capital crimes or crimes that lead to Title III sex offender status with a life sentence, can make you ineligible².

Additionally, if your condition is a direct result of wilful misconduct or occurred during a period of dishonorable conduct, the VA will not grant service connection for that condition³.

In summary:

- A bad conduct discharge that is not dishonorable may still allow eligibility for VA benefits¹.
- A dishonorable discharge generally bars eligibility².
- Conditions resulting from wilful misconduct or during dishonorable conduct are not service-connected³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Eligibility - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/upgrade)  
² [Eligibility - (notes)](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)  
³ [Wilful Misconduct or Dishonorable Conduct - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/norate)

### Question 271: EDGE-3

**Query:** Can I get benefits with an other than honorable discharge?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 5.17s

**Answer:**

Whether you can get benefits with an Other than Honorable discharge depends on the specific benefit and circumstances:

- For GI Bill education benefits, if you earned the GI Bill from a period of honorable service, you can keep the benefit even if you later receive an Other than Honorable discharge¹. Also, if you reenlisted or had a prior honorable period of service, you may still qualify for the GI Bill, though benefits may be prorated if the honorable service period was shorter than needed for full entitlement².

- For VA disability benefits, generally your character of discharge must be Honorable (including General, Under Honorable) to be eligible. However, even if your discharge was not Honorable, you may still be eligible in some cases, especially if you have a chronic disability related to service⁴. Also, the Veteran's last character of discharge must have been Honorable for eligibility through their own or a relative's service⁷.

- If you have an Other than Honorable discharge and a mental health diagnosis, you should apply to have your discharge upgraded, which may improve eligibility for benefits³.

In summary, an Other than Honorable discharge does not automatically disqualify you from all benefits, but eligibility varies by benefit type and circumstances. Applying for a discharge upgrade may be advisable if you have a mental health condition.

**Sources:**
¹ [GI Bill Education Benefits - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/gibill#faq)

⁷ [Eligibility - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com)

### Question 272: EDGE-4

**Query:** What if I was dishonorably discharged?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 3.19s

**Answer:**

If you were dishonorably discharged, the VA will **NOT** grant service connection for any condition that is a direct result of wilful misconduct or occurred during a period of dishonorable conduct. This includes conditions established while in AWOL or deserter status, drunk driving incidents, or other misconduct-related injuries.

Additionally, a dishonorable discharge generally makes a veteran ineligible for VA benefits, including disability compensation and GI Bill education benefits, as eligibility requires that the character of discharge was **NOT** dishonorable⁵. 

If you have a dishonorable discharge, you would likely need to seek a discharge upgrade to access VA benefits, but the context does not provide specific guidance on that process for dishonorable discharges (only mentions upgrades for Other than Honorable discharges with mental health diagnoses)¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Wilful Misconduct or Dishonorable Conduct - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/norate)

### Question 273: EDGE-5

**Query:** Can reservists get VA disability?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 4.45s

**Answer:**

Yes, reservists can get VA disability compensation if they incur or aggravate an injury, illness, or disease while in the line of duty, such as during Active Duty for Training (ADT), Inactive Duty Training (IADT), drill weekends, or any other training while on orders. This also includes traveling directly to or from their place of duty. To properly file a disability claim, reservists should have their unit issue a Line of Duty (LOD) determination or a Notice of Eligibility (NOE) if in the U.S. Coast Guard Reserve. If they do not obtain an LOD/NOE, they may rely on buddy letters, VA Form 21-10210, or personal statements as evidence. Pre- and post-deployment physicals can also serve as evidence if accurate and complete¹.

Additionally, reservists who had part-time Servicemembers' Group Life Insurance (SGLI) and suffered an injury or disability while on duty, or those within certain time frames of release or retirement from active duty or reserve status, may also meet eligibility criteria for benefits².

**Sources:**
¹ [National Guard/Reserves - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim#top)  
² [Eligibility - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

### Question 275: EDGE-7

**Query:** What is character of discharge?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 1.66s

**Answer:**

The "character of discharge" refers to the classification of a veteran's discharge status from military service, which can affect their eligibility for VA benefits. It is a formal designation that describes the nature of the discharge, such as Honorable, Other than Honorable, or other types. This classification is important because certain types of discharges may bar or limit access to VA benefits¹.

### Question 276: EDGE-8

**Query:** Can I get VA benefits if I never deployed?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 3.82s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get VA benefits even if you never deployed, as long as you meet certain eligibility criteria. For VA disability benefits, you must have a chronic disability related to your military service, a preexisting condition worsened by service, or a service-connected disability. Additionally, your character of discharge must be Honorable or General Under Honorable, and you must have served on Active Duty, Active Duty for training, or Inactive Duty Training¹.

For VA health care under the PACT Act, eligibility is based on meeting basic service and discharge requirements, and exposure to certain hazards or service in specific conflicts, but deployment itself is not strictly required⁶.

For education benefits like VEAP, you must have served and contributed to the program, but deployment is not a stated requirement⁷.

Therefore, deployment is not a mandatory condition to receive VA benefits; service and discharge status are key factors.

**Sources:**
¹ [Eligibility - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/upgrade)  
⁶ [Am I eligible for VA health care under the PACT Act? - (presumptive)](https://www.va.gov/resources/the-pact-act-and-your-va-benefits/#amieligibleforvahealthcareunderthepactact)  
⁷ [Chapter 32 (Veteran Education Assistance Program - VEAP) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/gibills#chapter32veteraneduc)

### Question 277: EDGE-9

**Query:** What if my condition started after service?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 3.62s

**Answer:**

If your condition started after service, it may still be possible to get service connection if your service-connected condition aggravated an existing post-service condition. To establish this, the VA must determine:

- The level of severity of the condition before aggravation; and  
- The level of severity after aggravation.

You must provide medical evidence showing the severity before aggravation or as soon as possible after aggravation but before the current severity level. The VA will then obtain a medical opinion to decide if your service-connected condition worsened the post-service condition beyond its natural progression. If the opinion is favorable, you can be granted service connection by aggravation.

If your condition is not related to aggravation but started after service, the context does not provide specific information about direct service connection for conditions starting post-service unrelated to aggravation.

**Sources:**  
¹ [Congenital or Genetic Conditions - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/norate)  
² [Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition - (claims_evidence)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofanexistingpostservicecondition)

### Question 278: EDGE-10

**Query:** Can I get benefits for conditions that existed before service?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 2.6s

**Answer:**

You generally cannot get VA disability benefits for conditions that existed before your service if they are congenital or genetic (conditions you were born with), such as male pattern baldness, personality disorders, or poor eyesight. However, there are exceptions:

- If the condition first manifests during your service after the time medical science says it should have developed, it can be rated.
- If the condition worsened (was aggravated) due to your service or a service-connected condition, it can be rated.
- For Active Duty service members who served at least 8 years, there may be additional considerations related to DoD Medboards.

So, pre-existing conditions can be eligible for benefits only if they meet these exceptions related to manifestation or aggravation during service¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Congenital or Genetic Conditions - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/norate)

### Question 279: EDGE-11

**Query:** What is pre-existing condition aggravation?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 5.65s

**Answer:**

Pre-existing condition aggravation refers to the worsening of a condition that existed before military service due to service or a service-connected condition. There are two main types:

1. **Aggravation of a Preexisting (Pre-service) Condition:** This occurs when military service worsens a condition that existed before service. The VA presumes a veteran was "sound" (without condition) at entry unless the condition was noted during the Military Entrance Processing Station (MEPS) exam or is a chronic condition diagnosed shortly after service that likely existed before service. Medical evidence is required to prove the condition existed prior to service. Statements alone from the veteran or others do not carry weight without medical evidence. If the Department of Defense lost entrance physical records, the VA assumes no preexisting condition unless exceptions apply.

2. **Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition:** This is when a service-connected condition worsens a condition that developed after service. To establish aggravation, the VA must determine the severity of the condition before and after aggravation and obtain a medical opinion that the service-connected condition worsened the existing condition beyond its natural progression¹.

In rating aggravated conditions, the VA subtracts the preexisting condition's severity from the current severity to determine the aggravated rating. For preexisting (pre-service) conditions, if the preexisting condition rates 100%, the final rating remains 100%. If the VA cannot determine the preexisting severity, it assumes 0% severity¹³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Aggravation of a Preexisting (Pre-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofapreexistingpreservicecondition)  
² [Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofanexistingpostservicecondition)  
³ [Rating Aggravated Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#ratingaggravatedconditions)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofanexistingpostservicecondition)
- [Aggravation of a Preexisting (Pre-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofapreexistingpreservicecondition)
- [Rating Aggravated Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#ratingaggravatedconditions)
- [Service-Connection by Aggravation](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#serviceconnectionbyaggravation)
- [Service-Connection by Aggravation - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#references)
- [Common Secondary Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype#secondary)
- [38 USC 1151 Claims (VA Negligence) - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#faq)

### Question 280: EDGE-12

**Query:** Can I get benefits for genetic conditions?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 2.28s

**Answer:**

Generally, congenital or genetic conditions (those you were born with) are not rated for VA benefits. However, there are exceptions where such conditions can be rated:

- If the condition manifests during your service after the time period medical science has determined it should have developed.
- If the condition worsened due to your service or a service-connected condition (aggravated).
- For Active Duty service members who serve at least 8 years (this applies only to DoD Medboards).

So, you may be eligible for benefits if your genetic condition meets one of these exceptions¹.

### Question 282: EDGE-14

**Query:** Can I file a claim without medical records?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 4.75s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **49.9%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

You should not file a claim without having the bare minimum evidence. Before filing a claim, you need to have all three of the following parts:

- Statement of symptoms (how the condition affects you) or current diagnosis.
- An in-service event (documented or supported by lay statements) showing the condition started in service, or a service-connected disability that caused or worsened your disability.
- A nexus, meaning a layperson would think that the symptoms or diagnosis and the in-service event are related.

If your claim does not have all three parts, you should not file it because it will be denied without even being sent to a Compensation & Pension Exam (C&P)¹.

Regarding medical records specifically, you can provide private medical records or non-VA hospital reports as evidence, but if you want the VA to obtain them for you, you must provide a VA Form 21-4142/21-4142a. The VA can also access your VHA medical records themselves, so it is not necessary to upload them as part of your claim².

In summary, while you might not need to submit all medical records yourself, you must have sufficient evidence including symptoms or diagnosis, in-service event, and nexus before filing a claim. Filing without these will likely result in denial.

**Sources:**
¹ [BARE MINIMUM You Need BEFORE You File a Claim: - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
² [Miscellaneous Evidence - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 283: EDGE-15

**Query:** What if my doctor won't write a nexus letter?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 2.08s

**Answer:**

If your doctor won't write a nexus letter, it may be due to their personal or professional policy or because they want to be properly compensated for their time and work. You should make it clear that you will compensate them appropriately, as writing a nexus letter involves reviewing your records, doing relevant research, and typing up the opinion, which can cost at least a thousand dollars or more. If they are still unwilling, you will need to seek out another physician who is willing to provide the letter¹.

**Sources:**

### Question 284: EDGE-16

**Query:** Can I get benefits for conditions not in my service record?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 4.05s

**Answer:**

To receive VA disability benefits for conditions not documented in your service record, you must meet certain criteria:

- You need to provide a statement of symptoms or a current diagnosis.
- You must show an in-service event or service-connected disability that caused or worsened your condition.
- There must be a nexus (connection) between the in-service event and your current condition, such that a layperson would reasonably think they are related.

If your condition is not in your service record, you can still file a claim if you can establish these three elements through lay statements, medical evidence, or other documentation. Without all three, your claim will likely be denied without even being sent for a Compensation & Pension Exam (C&P)¹.

Additionally, if your condition is congenital or genetic (something you were born with), it generally cannot be rated unless it manifested during service after the expected development period or was aggravated by service².

Also, private medical records outside your military treatment records are not automatically included in your service record. You must coordinate with your base medical to have those records added if you want them considered in your claim³.

In summary, you can get benefits for conditions not in your service record if you can establish a service connection with sufficient evidence and meet the VA's claim requirements.

**Sources:**
¹ [BARE MINIMUM You Need BEFORE You File a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

³ [Active Duty Servicemembers - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)

### Question 288: EDGE-20

**Query:** Can I request a different C&P examiner?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 1.68s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can request a different C&P examiner or a specific contractor or VA medical center for your Compensation and Pension examination. You can include a personal statement with your request, and the VA will attempt to honor it if it is reasonable and possible. Additionally, you can request a tele-health appointment for your mental health C&P exam if you prefer¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Compensation & Pension Examinations (C&P)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)
